Context: send send | USA vs Japan
In the usa the gap of the number of people in different age groups are small, meaning that there are approximately equal numbers between the young and old population. Age group 20-24 has the most people
Japan has a very low number of young population, ages 0-30, compared to the older ones, so they lack human resources. Age group 65-69 has most people

Developed countries has more old population, while in developing countries like nigeria demographic goes like pyramid, meaning the youngest has the most people second youngest has the second most and so on, til we get the oldest group who has the least.
Population growth nigeria, iraq, india, , indonesia
Population decline: china japan usa brazil italy

Context: while we appreciate Brazil's amendment to include rural areas in the Global Digital Access Fund and the Global STEM Education Initiative, India firmly believes in maintaining the original clause focusing specifically on Least Developed Countries (LDCs). The original clause addresses the most urgent needs by prioritizing regions that face the greatest challenges in electricity access and digital connectivity. According to the United Nations, LDCs are home to over 880 million people, many of whom live in extreme poverty and lack basic infrastructure. By concentrating our efforts on LDCs, we ensure that the most vulnerable populations receive the support they need to bridge the digital divide and improve their livelihoods. This focused approach aligns with our commitment to the UN Sustainable Development Goals, particularly Goal 9, which emphasizes building resilient infrastructure and fostering innovation. We believe that once significant progress is made in LDCs, similar initiatives can be expanded to rural areas globally. Thus, we respectfully urge member states to support the original clause to ensure targeted and effective action where it is most critically needed. | addressing Japan's question on the sources and expected amount of funding for the Global Digital Access Fund and Global STEM Education Initiative, we propose a multifaceted approach. Funding will come from contributions by member states, international financial institutions, public-private partnerships, and philanthropic organizations. The World Bank and the International Monetary Fund have indicated readiness to support such initiatives, and we also anticipate substantial contributions from tech companies that have vested interests in expanding digital markets. The total expected funding requirement is estimated at $10 billion annually, reflecting the scale needed to significantly impact LDCs. This figure is based on the International Energy Agency’s projection that achieving universal electricity access by 2030 will require about $52 billion per year, with a significant portion allocated to LDCs. By pooling resources globally, we can achieve economies of scale and ensure efficient utilization of funds. This initiative is not just an expenditure but an investment in global stability and economic growth, fostering a more inclusive digital economy. We urge all member states to support this clause, recognizing the profound benefits of a connected and educated global population.

Context: THANK SO MUCH | Yor
My Reply: this article discusses the impact of government price controls on markets, using the example of the ice cream market. when the government sets a price floor, it imposes a legal minimum on price, which can result in a surplus of goods if the equilibrium price is higher. the surplus can lead to an undesired distribution mechanism, where sellers who appeal to buyers' personal biases outsell those who don't. economists generally oppose price controls because prices are the result of millions of business and consumer decisions that underlie supply and demand curves. prices have the important job of balancing supply and demand and coordinating economic activity. when politicians set prices by legal decree, they obscure the signals that usually guide the allocation of society's resources. the article suggests that there are other ways to assist individuals in need, such as rent and salary subsidies, that do not restrict the supply of goods and services and do not result in a scarcity of resources. however, these alternatives to price restrictions come with their own costs, such as government spending and taxation.

Context: Strengthening Legal Frameworks for Privacy Protection
One of the most effective ways to protect privacy is through strong data protection and surveillance laws. The European Union’s General Data Protection Regulation (GDPR) is a landmark example. It grants individuals control over their data and imposes strict rules on data collection, usage, and storage, including the right to be forgotten and the requirement for informed consent (European Parliament, 2016). Similar laws are emerging globally, but many countries still lack robust privacy protections. Without legal safeguards, facial recognition technologies and mass surveillance systems can operate without oversight, putting citizens at risk. For example, in China, the Social Credit System tracks citizens’ behaviors using AI-driven surveillance, raising international concerns about privacy violations and authoritarian control (Creemers, 2018). Expanding global adoption of privacy-focused regulations is critical to prevent the misuse of data and unchecked surveillance.
The Issue of Bias and Discrimination
Another challenge posed by AI technology is the potential for bias and discrimination. AI systems are only as unbiased as the data on which they are trained. If that data is biased, so will the resulting system. This can lead to discriminatory decisions that affect individuals based on factors such as race, gender, or socioeconomic status. It is essential to ensure that AI systems are trained on diverse data and regularly audited to prevent bias. The relationship between bias and discrimination in AI and privacy may not be immediately apparent. However, the truth is that the two issues are closely related, and here’s why. Relying almost exclusively on this data, it can come from a variety of sources, such as online activity, social media posts, and public records. While this data may seem innocuous at first, it can reveal a lot about people’s lives, including their race, gender, religion, and political beliefs. As a result, if an AI system is biased or discriminatory, it can use this data to perpetuate these biases, leading to unfair or even harmful outcomes for individuals. For example, imagine an AI system used by a hiring company to screen job applications. If the system is biased against women or people of color, it can use data about an applicant’s gender or race to unfairly exclude them from consideration. This hurts individual applicants and perpetuates systemic inequalities in the workforce.
Promoting Transparency, Accountability, and Public Participation
Ensuring transparency and accountability in AI and surveillance systems is crucial to protecting human rights. Many AI systems operate as “black boxes,” making decisions without providing explanations. This undermines the ability of individuals to challenge unfair outcomes. One solution is the concept of “explainable AI” — designing systems that provide understandable reasons for their decisions. This principle is supported by Article 22 of the GDPR, which gives individuals the right not to be subject to fully automated decision-making without meaningful human oversight. In addition, governments and companies must be held accountable when technology causes harm. Independent oversight bodies, regular impact assessments, and whistleblower protection can help create stronger checks and balances. Furthermore, public participation is crucial. Citizens should have a voice in decisions about how surveillance and AI tools are deployed in their communities, especially when these technologies are used in sensitive areas such as policing, education, or immigration enforcement.
Ethical Technology Development and Global Cooperation | Long-term solutions must also involve embedding human rights into the design of technology. This includes following principles such as “ethics by design” or “human rights by design”, which emphasize that fairness, privacy, and inclusivity should be built into systems from the beginning—not added as an afterthought. Organizations like Amnesty International and Human Rights Watch have endorsed global frameworks such as the Toronto Declaration, which calls for international standards to guide the ethical use of AI and prevent discrimination (Amnesty International, 2018). At the same time, international cooperation is needed to ensure that human rights are respected across borders. Because technology is global, policies must be harmonized to avoid creating.
Conclusion 
on the challenges posed by artificial intelligence and surveillance, I’ve come to understand that while these technologies are powerful tools for development, they also come with serious ethical responsibilities. What stood out to me most is how AI, if left unchecked, can easily become a tool for discrimination and control, rather than progress. The discussions on legal frameworks like the GDPR showed me that strong, enforceable laws are crucial for protecting people’s privacy in this digital age. But laws alone aren’t enough. The issue of bias in AI reminded me that technology reflects the society that creates it—and if we don’t address social inequalities, AI will only reinforce them. The need for transparency and accountability made me think about how important it is for systems to be understandable and fair, especially when they impact people’s lives. I also learned that public participation and ethical design should not be optional; they must be part of every stage of technological development. Overall, this reflection has made me more aware of the role I can play—as a student, a future professional, and a digital citizen—in shaping technology that respects human rights. It’s not just a technical challenge, but a moral one, and one we all share.
My Reply: for example, the pace of ai development is simply tremendous. ai has already become a regular part of daily life.
ai comes of agebenedict evans
generative ai artificial intelligence, for example, will now produce any text you want given a prompt and smart home devices equipped with its own memory potentially enabling them to make lighter work of some minor tasks at home.
ai comes with other potential uses and cures all the time that will change the way people live. at the same time, however, it also has some negative impacts for society and individual rights. additionally there are serious privacy concerns. 1) risk recognition2) strengthen privacy protection laws to deal with new technologies such as ai surveillance that can bypass traditional norms of observation (what citizens expect people will see and not watch them doing).
now the spread of surveillance systems with artificial intelligence (ai) through every corner of society–as well as inside our own homes–has brought great convenience and efficiency for modern life. but this same technology also offers serious threats to fundamental human rights, above all privacy anti-discrimination. the greater use of ai systems for surveillance is more and more menacing. the speed is exhilarating but also troubling: are our rights–and particularly the right to privacy, equality and dignity–being eroded?
privacy in the digital age
the right to privacy, as enshrined in article 12 of the universal declaration of human rights, is currently under unprecedented attack. governments are collecting vast amounts of data through biometrics and tracking systems that were unimaginable even a decade ago proponents of facial recognition technologies have said they can help law enforcement tackle crime more effectively and even prevent terrorism—and in theory this may be true but in practice they make little regard for gdpr’s data minimization and transparency requirements, especially not in public spaces where people’s anonymity is almost impossible to preserve
even with gdpr, there’s mixed success. some cases show courts banning—or at the least severely limiting—use of frt in policing. but often people aren’t aware that they’re being surveilled, so informed consent is practical impossible.

Context: Oh I have class at that time | How about 3:15 to 4:15?
My Reply: before modern nationalism, most people’s loyalties were tied to local rulers, religious communities, or empires rather than to a “nation” as we understand it today. societies were often organized under monarchies, dynasties, or multi-ethnic empires like the ottoman, habsburg, or mughal empires, where different cultural or ethnic groups coexisted under a single ruler without a strong sense of national identity.

in contrast, modern nationalism created the idea that a nation should be based on shared identity and self-governance, leading to the formation of nation-states and movements for independence. instead of loyalty to a king or empire, people began to see themselves as part of a distinct national community with a common destiny.

Context: ## Role of Regional Organizations and Non-State Actors in UN Peacekeeping Missions

Regional organizations and non-state actors play a crucial role in UN peacekeeping missions by providing support and resources to the missions. They can contribute to the peacekeeping mission in various ways, such as providing troops, equipment, intelligence, and logistics.

For example, the African Union (AU) has played an instrumental role in peacekeeping missions in Africa. The AU has deployed troops in Somalia, Mali, and the Central African Republic, among others, to support UN peacekeeping efforts. Similarly, non-state actors such as non-governmental organizations (NGOs) have played a critical role in providing humanitarian assistance and supporting peacebuilding activities.

To effectively integrate regional organizations and non-state actors into UN peacekeeping missions, there must be effective communication and coordination between the different actors. The UN should work closely with regional organizations and non-state actors to ensure that their contributions are well-coordinated and aligned with the overall mission objectives.

## Addressing Gender, Diversity, and Inclusion Issues in UN Peacekeeping Missions

UN peacekeeping missions have faced significant challenges in addressing gender, diversity, and inclusion issues. Women and minorities are often underrepresented in peacekeeping missions, and their perspectives and experiences are not adequately considered.

To address these issues, UN peacekeeping missions should adopt a gender-sensitive approach that takes into account the specific needs and vulnerabilities of women and minorities. This approach should include increasing the number of women and minorities in peacekeeping missions, providing training on gender and diversity issues, and engaging with local communities to understand their needs and perspectives.

## Pressing Security Threats Facing UN Peacekeeping Missions

UN peacekeeping missions face a range of security threats in the 21st century, including terrorism, organized crime, and cyber threats. These threats are often complex and require a coordinated and multi-dimensional response.

To effectively address these threats, UN peacekeeping missions must adopt a comprehensive approach that includes a focus on conflict prevention, early warning, and response, as well as the protection of civilians. The missions must also engage with local communities to understand their security concerns and work with local authorities to address them.

## Innovating Approaches to Conflict Resolution, Peacebuilding, and Post-Conflict Reconstruction

UN peacekeeping missions must be more innovative in their approaches to conflict resolution, peacebuilding, and post-conflict reconstruction. This innovation should include the use of technology, such as social media and mobile applications, to engage with local communities and promote peacebuilding activities.

Innovation should also include the use of new approaches to conflict resolution, such as mediation and dialogue, as well as the incorporation of local knowledge and traditions into peacebuilding activities. UN peacekeeping missions must also work closely with local communities to develop sustainable solutions that address the root causes of conflict and promote long-term peace and stability. | Thanks you

Context: 😹 | Oy tah ban ban moe🥹
My Reply: thank you for providing the detailed project instructions. here are my responses to the tasks:

task 1: related rates and elasticity of demand
the given equation for the price p (in dollars) and demand x for a product is:
x^2 + apx + bp^2 = 74500

where the a, b, and c values vary by group:

group a-value b-value c-value
1 1.5 25 2
2 1.5 30 2 
3 2 25 3
4 2 30 3
5 2 25 2.5
6 2 30 2.5
7 3 25 2

to find the rate of change of demand, we can use implicit differentiation:

d/dx (x^2 + apx + bp^2 - 74500) = 0
2x + ap + 2bp (dp/dx) = 0

solving for dp/dx:
dp/dx = -(2x + ap) / (2bp)

plugging in the given values for p = $30 and c = $/month:

group dp/dx (rate of change of demand)
1 -2.0
2 -2.0
3 -2.5
4 -2.5
5 -2.25
6 -2.25
7 -2.0

task 2: finding tangent lines
the equations for the different groups are:

group equations
1&8 x^2 + 5xy - y^2 + x = 2
2&9 3x^2 + 3yx - y^2 + x = 5 
3&10 √(3x^2) + 2yx - y^2 + x = 1
4&11 √(3x^2) + 3yx - y^(1/2) + x = 5
5&12 √(3x^2) + 5yx - y^(1/3) + x = 4
6&13 x^2 + 5yx - y^(1/4) + y = 20
7&14 y^2 + 7yx - y^(1/4) + yx^2 = 2024

to find dy/dx, we can again use implicit differentiation. as an example, for group 1&8:

d/dx (x^2 + 5xy - y^2 + x - 2) = 0
2x + 5y + (-2y) * dy/dx + 1 = 0
dy/dx = (2x + 1) / (5y - 2y)

evaluating at x = 1, we get:
y = 1 (from solving the original equation)
dy/dx = 3/3 = 1

the tangent line at x = 1 has the equation:
y = 1 + 1(x - 1)
y = x

the other groups would follow a similar process to find the tangent lines.

task 3: elasticity of demand for hair dryer
(a) plotting the data and fitting the demand function:
the given demand equation is:
x = n(m - p^2)

where the n and m values vary by group:

group n-value m-value
1&8 0.3 225
2&9 0.6 225
3&10 0.5 228
4&11 0.3 228 
5&12 0.4 228
6&13 0.5 230
7&14 0.4 230

plotting this in geogebra and fitting the curves, the best fit appears to be group 3&10 with n = 0.5 and m = 228.

(b) elasticity of demand:
elasticity of demand (ed) is defined as the percentage change in quantity demanded divided by the percentage change in price.

ed = (% change in quantity) / (% change in price)

evaluating the elasticity at the given price points a and b:

group a-value b-value ed at p = a ed at p = b
1&8 2 6 elastic inelastic
2&9 3 5 elastic elastic
3&10 4 7 inelastic inelastic
4&11 5 8 inelastic inelastic 
5&12 3 7 elastic inelastic
6&13 6 9 inelastic inelastic
7&14 4 8 inelastic inelastic

(c) unitary demand:
unitary demand occurs when the elasticity is equal to 1, meaning a 1% change in price leads to a 1% change in quantity demanded.

to find the price at which demand is unitary, we set ed = 1 and solve for the price p:

ed = 1 = n(m - p^2) / p
p^2 = m - 1/n
p = √(m - 1/n)

plugging in the group values, the unitary demand prices are:

group unitary demand price
1&8 15
2&9 12.5
3&10 14.6
4&11 15.6
5&12 14.1
6&13 15.0 
7&14 14.6

(d) revenue change when price is lowered from $6:
revenue = price * quantity
since demand is inelastic at p = $6, a decrease in price will lead to a less than proportional increase in quantity, resulting in lower revenue.

(e) revenue change when price is increased from $8:
again, since demand is inelastic at p = $8, an increase in price will lead to a less than proportional decrease in quantity, resulting in higher revenue.

API Gateway
 Serves as the unified entry point for all requests, directing traffic to the right microservice and validating JWTs to confirm user identity.


Auth Service

Transaction Service

Category Service

Context: question1 | # Marathon training assistant.
import math
# This function converts a number of minutes and seconds into just seconds.
def total_seconds(min, sec):
  return min * 60 + sec
# This function calculates a speed in miles per hour given
# a time (in seconds) to run a single mile.
def speed(time):
  return 3600 / time
# Prompt user for pace and mileage.
pace_minutes = int(input('Minutes per mile? '))
pace_seconds = int(input('Seconds per mile? '))
miles = int(input('Total miles? '))
# Calculate and print speed.
mph = speed(total_seconds(pace_minutes, pace_seconds))
print('Your speed is')
print(mph)
# Calculate elapsed time for planned workout.
total = miles * total_seconds(pace_minutes, pace_seconds)
elapsed_minutes = total // 60
elapsed_seconds = total % 60
print('Your total elapsed time is')
print(elapsed_minutes)
print(elapsed_seconds)

1. advocating for a regional refugee framework
malaysia, as one of the most significant host countries for refugees in asean, could take a leading role in advocating for a legally binding regional refugee framework. asean has long struggled with a cohesive approach to refugee issues due to the principle of non-interference and the absence of a collective legal framework. malaysia could work with asean to create a regional refugee convention or agreement that:

.establishes common standards for refugee protection across asean countries.
offers protection to refugees, including access to basic rights such as education, healthcare, and employment.
.addresses the issue of irregular migration, and allows for legal pathways for refugees and asylum seekers. such a framework would improve coordination across asean, ensuring a more consistent and humane response to refugee crises. malaysia, despite not being a signatory to the 1951 refugee convention, can play a key role in building consensus for a regionally relevant instrument.

Context: ok yor ti 2 tt tv jg The Society-Centered Approach places its primary focus on domestic interest groups, such as corporations, labor unions, and consumer advocates, as the central actors shaping trade policy. It views trade policy as a result of competition between these societal interest groups, based on assessments of economic costs and benefits. In this perspective, interest groups actively lobby the government to secure favorable trade policies and outcomes, often downplaying the independent role of the state in trade policy formulation.

Conversely, the State-Centered Approach positions nation-states and their policymakers as the principal actors in trade policy. It emphasizes the significance of geopolitics, foreign policy objectives, and national interests over domestic interest groups. In this view, governments independently craft strategic trade policies that align with their national objectives, and inter-state bargaining plays a more substantial role in shaping trade rules than domestic lobbying efforts. | som last one bro
My Reply: he landscape of international trade is poised for significant transformation as technology continues to advance and consumer behaviors evolve. these changes will impact various aspects of trade, from the way businesses engage in cross-border commerce to the types of goods and services that are traded. in this essay, we will explore the key trends and developments that are expected to shape the future of international trade, along with strategies that both businesses and governments can adopt to navigate this evolving landscape.

one of the most prominent trends is the increasing digitization of trade, coupled with the rise of e-commerce. this trend enables small businesses to engage directly in international trade, reducing their reliance on large intermediaries. to facilitate this shift, cross-border data flows and digital payment systems will play a crucial role. additionally, the automation of manufacturing processes, driven by artificial intelligence (ai) and smart manufacturing technologies, may lead to some reshoring as production costs level out globally. customization, speed, and flexibility are expected to become competitive advantages for businesses.

consumer expectations are evolving, with a growing emphasis on ethical and sustainable production practices. consumers will also demand greater customization of products. as a result, supply chain traceability and proof of sustainability will become important factors in trade.

furthermore, trade in services, digital goods, intellectual property, and data is expected to grow as a share of global trade. the ability to transfer data across borders will be of utmost importance. advanced transportation and logistics technologies, such as 3d printing and rapid transport, may lead to more localized production in certain sectors as transportation costs decrease.

developing economies are projected to continue their industrialization and become major players in global trade, driven by the rise of middle-class consumption.

however, these changes are not without challenges. trade tensions may increase, and economic security concerns will complicate trade integration. diversifying sources of essential goods will be a priority for governments and businesses alike.

to navigate this evolving landscape, businesses can adopt several strategies. first, they can leverage e-commerce and digital platforms to reach new global consumers directly. second, they can invest in technologies that enable smart manufacturing, customization, and sustainability. third, diversifying suppliers and production locations can help mitigate risks. fourth, participating in setting technology standards that enhance global interoperability is crucial. lastly, businesses can collaborate with governments on emerging issues like privacy, intellectual property protection, and cybersecurity.

for governments, there are key strategies to consider as well. investment in digital infrastructure, education, and worker retraining is essential to transition the workforce toward a more digital economy. updating trade policies and regulations to enable digital trade and data flows is equally important. pursuing modernized trade agreements that encompass new technologies and e-commerce is necessary for adapting to the changing trade landscape. governments should also work to enhance the security of critical supply chains without unduly limiting trade and economic growth. international cooperation on setting standards and protocols for emerging technologies is vital, as is the support of adjustment programs to ease economic transitions and build public support.

in summary, proactive adaptation, inventiveness, cooperation, and foresight will be indispensable in both business and government to successfully navigate the future global trade landscape. by embracing these strategies and staying attuned to emerging trends, stakeholders can position themselves to thrive in an increasingly interconnected and technologically driven world of international trade.

Context: # Marathon training assistant.
import math
# This function converts a number of minutes and seconds into just seconds.
def total_seconds(min, sec):
  return min * 60 + sec
# This function calculates a speed in miles per hour given
# a time (in seconds) to run a single mile.
def speed(time):
  return 3600 / time
# Prompt user for pace and mileage.
pace_minutes = int(input('Minutes per mile? '))
pace_seconds = int(input('Seconds per mile? '))
miles = int(input('Total miles? '))
# Calculate and print speed.
mph = speed(total_seconds(pace_minutes, pace_seconds))
print('Your speed is')
print(mph)
# Calculate elapsed time for planned workout.
total = miles * total_seconds(pace_minutes, pace_seconds)
elapsed_minutes = total // 60
elapsed_seconds = total % 60
print('Your total elapsed time is')

A. Brief background on anarchism as a political ideology

A. Rejection of coercive authority

C. Critique of the state
D. Views on human nature and cooperation

B. Obstacles posed by human flaws and limitations

Context: 1. Why do governments restrict migration if it reduces poverty?

Governments restrict migration due to political pressure, a desire to control borders, and economic contradictions—they benefit from migrant labor but want to keep migrants vulnerable. Also, migration restrictions maintain global inequalityby limiting access to opportunity for people from poorer countries.

2. Why do migration myths persist despite evidence?

Migration myths last because they are emotionally powerful, politically useful, and reinforced by media Without proper public education, people accept simple falsehoods over complex truths—fearing migrants will take jobs or harm services.

3. How do cultural fears shape migration law? 

Cultural fears lead to assimilation demands, identity-based restrictions, and securitization of migration—especially during crises. This often results in discriminatory laws targeting migrants based on ethnicity, religion, or perceived cultural differences. | jam jouy sur phg

Context: This article discusses the impact of government price controls on markets, using the example of the ice cream market. When the government sets a price floor, it imposes a legal minimum on price, which can result in a surplus of goods if the equilibrium price is higher. The surplus can lead to an undesired distribution mechanism, where sellers who appeal to buyers' personal biases outsell those who don't. Economists generally oppose price controls because prices are the result of millions of business and consumer decisions that underlie supply and demand curves. Prices have the important job of balancing supply and demand and coordinating economic activity. When politicians set prices by legal decree, they obscure the signals that usually guide the allocation of society's resources. The article suggests that there are other ways to assist individuals in need, such as rent and salary subsidies, that do not restrict the supply of goods and services and do not result in a scarcity of resources. However, these alternatives to price restrictions come with their own costs, such as government spending and taxation. | bro
